class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Culture_of_England" title="Template:Culture of England"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Culture_of_England" title="Template talk:Culture of England"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>culture of England</b> is defined by the idiosyncratic <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> norms of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English people</a>. \n</p><p>Owing to England\'s influential position within the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> it can sometimes be difficult to differentiate English culture from the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Culture of the United Kingdom">culture of the United Kingdom</a> as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> However, since Anglo-Saxon times, England has had its own unique culture, apart from Welsh, Scottish or Northern Irish culture.\n</p><p>Humour, tradition and good manners are characteristics commonly associated with being English.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Celebration of Christmas</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Historical_markers:_blue_plaques"><span Revolution</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Sport_and_leisure"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Sport and leisure</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Symbols"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Symbols</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">20</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture_and_gardens">Architecture and gardens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Architecture and gardens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_historic_houses_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="List of historic houses in England">List of historic houses in England</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_Heritage_properties" title="List of English Heritage properties">List of English Heritage properties</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Trust_properties_in_England" title="List of National Trust properties in England">List of National Trust properties in England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/220px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/330px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/440px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, London, is an example of <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a></div></div></div>\n<p>English architecture begins with the <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Anglo-Saxons" class="mw-redirect" title="Architecture of the Anglo-Saxons">architecture of the Anglo-Saxons</a>. At least fifty surviving English churches are of Anglo-Saxon origin, although in some cases the Anglo-Saxon part is small and much-altered. All except one timber church are built of stone or brick, and in some cases show evidence of reused Roman work. The architectural character of Anglo-Saxon ecclesiastical buildings ranges from <a href="/wiki/Coptic_architecture" title="Coptic architecture">Coptic</a>-influenced architecture in the early period, through Early Christian <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> influenced architecture, to (in the later Anglo-Saxon period) an architecture characterized by <a href="/wiki/Pilaster" title="Pilaster">pilaster</a>-strips, blank <a href="/wiki/Arcade_(architecture)" title="Arcade (architecture)">arcading</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baluster" title="Baluster">baluster</a> shafts and triangular-headed openings. Almost no secular work remains above ground.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg/220px-Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg/330px-Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg/440px-Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chatsworth_showing_hunting_tower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chatsworth_House" title="Chatsworth House">Chatsworth House</a>, a famous example of an <a href="/wiki/English_country_house" title="English country house">English country house</a> surrounded by an <a href="/wiki/English_garden" class="mw-redirect" title="English garden">English garden</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Following the Norman Conquest Romanesque architecture (known here as Norman architecture) superseded Anglo-Saxon architecture; later there was a period of transition into English Gothic architecture (of which there are three periods, Early English, Decorated, and Perpendicular). In early modern times there was an influence from Renaissance architecture until by the 18th century Gothic forms of architecture had been abandoned and various classical styles were adopted. During the Victorian period Neo-Gothic architecture was preferred for many types of buildings but this did not continue into the 20th century. \n</p><p>Other buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Cathedrals" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedrals">cathedrals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish churches</a> are associated with a sense of traditional Englishness, as is often the palatial \'<a href="/wiki/Stately_home" class="mw-redirect" title="Stately home">stately home</a>\'. Many people are interested in the <a href="/wiki/English_country_house" title="English country house">English country house</a> and the rural lifestyle, evidenced by the number of visitors to properties managed by <a href="/wiki/English_Heritage" title="English Heritage">English Heritage</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Trust_for_Places_of_Historic_Interest_or_Natural_Beauty" title="National Trust for Places of Historic Interest or Natural Beauty">National Trust</a>.\n</p><p>Landscape gardening as developed by <a href="/wiki/Capability_Brown" title="Capability Brown">Capability Brown</a> set an international trend for the <a href="/wiki/English_garden" class="mw-redirect" title="English garden">English garden</a>. Gardening, and visiting gardens, are regarded as typically English pursuits.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_seaside_piers">English seaside piers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: English seaside piers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brighton_Pier,_Brighton,_East_Sussex,_England-2Oct2011_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Brighton_Pier%2C_Brighton%2C_East_Sussex%2C_England-2Oct2011_%281%29.jpg/220px-Brighton_Pier%2C_Brighton%2C_East_Sussex%2C_England-2Oct2011_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Brighton_Pier%2C_Brighton%2C_East_Sussex%2C_England-2Oct2011_%281%29.jpg/330px-Brighton_Pier%2C_Brighton%2C_East_Sussex%2C_England-2Oct2011_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Brighton_Pier%2C_Brighton%2C_East_Sussex%2C_England-2Oct2011_%281%29.jpg/440px-Brighton_Pier%2C_Brighton%2C_East_Sussex%2C_England-2Oct2011_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2794" data-file-height="1871" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brighton_Pier,_Brighton,_East_Sussex,_England-2Oct2011_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seaside pleasure pier in <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a>. The first <a href="/wiki/Pier#Pleasure_piers" title="Pier">seaside piers</a> were built in England in the early 19th century</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the building of the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Pier#Pleasure_piers" title="Pier">seaside pier</a> in July 1814 in <a href="/wiki/Ryde" title="Ryde">Ryde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a> off the south coast of England, the pier became fashionable at <a href="/wiki/Seaside_resort" title="Seaside resort">seaside resorts</a> in England and Wales during the Victorian era, peaking in the 1860s with 69 being built.<sup id="cite_ref-seaside_pier_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seaside_pier-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Providing a walkway out to sea, the seaside pier is regarded among the finest Victorian architecture, and is an iconic symbol of the British seaside holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-seaside_pier_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seaside_pier-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pier_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pier-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> By 1914, more than 100 piers were located around the UK coast.<sup id="cite_ref-seaside_pier_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seaside_pier-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  Today there are approximately 55 <a href="/wiki/List_of_piers_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of piers in the United Kingdom">seaside piers in the UK</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_art" title="English art">English art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg/220px-John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg/330px-John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg/440px-John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2547" data-file-height="1756" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Constable_The_Hay_Wain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Hay Wain</i> by <a href="/wiki/John_Constable" title="John Constable">John Constable</a> is an archetypal English painting.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/English_art" title="English art">English art</a> was dominated by imported artists throughout much of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, but in the 18th century a native tradition became much admired. It is considered to be typified by <a href="/wiki/Landscape_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Landscape art">landscape painting</a>, such as the work of <a href="/wiki/J.M.W._Turner" class="mw-redirect" title="J.M.W. Turner">J.M.W. Turner</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Constable" title="John Constable">John Constable</a>. Portraitists like <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gainsborough" title="Thomas Gainsborough">Thomas Gainsborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Reynolds" title="Joshua Reynolds">Joshua Reynolds</a> are also significant.\n</p><p><br />\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A-voluptuary.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/A-voluptuary.jpg/220px-A-voluptuary.jpg" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/A-voluptuary.jpg/330px-A-voluptuary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/A-voluptuary.jpg/440px-A-voluptuary.jpg 2x" data-file-width="829" data-file-height="1076" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A-voluptuary.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Voluptuary under the horrors of Digestion (1792)</div></div></div>\n<p>Pictorial satirist <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a> pioneered Western sequential art, and political illustrations in this style are often referred to as "Hogarthian".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Following the work of Hogarth, <a href="/wiki/Political_cartoons" class="mw-redirect" title="Political cartoons">political cartoons</a> developed in England in the latter part of the 18th century under the direction of <a href="/wiki/James_Gillray" title="James Gillray">James Gillray</a>. Regarded as being one of the two most influential <a href="/wiki/Cartoonist" title="Cartoonist">cartoonists</a> (the other being Hogarth), Gillray has been referred to as the father of the political cartoon, with his satirical work calling the king (George III), prime ministers and generals to account.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_cuisine" title="English cuisine">English cuisine</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Englishbreakfast.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Englishbreakfast.jpg/220px-Englishbreakfast.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Englishbreakfast.jpg/330px-Englishbreakfast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Englishbreakfast.jpg/440px-Englishbreakfast.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Englishbreakfast.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A full English breakfast with fried egg, <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausage</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_pudding" title="White pudding">white</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_pudding" title="Black pudding">black pudding</a>, bacon, mushrooms, baked beans, <a href="/wiki/Hash_browns" title="Hash browns">hash browns</a>, toast, and half a tomato</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg/220px-ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg/330px-ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg/440px-ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1485" data-file-height="869" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristmasDinnerScotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British <a href="/wiki/Christmas_dinner" title="Christmas dinner">Christmas dinner</a> plate, featuring roast <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(bird)" title="Turkey (bird)">turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potatoes" class="mw-redirect" title="Potatoes">roast potatoes</a>, mashed potatoes and <a href="/wiki/Brussels_sprouts" class="mw-redirect" title="Brussels sprouts">Brussels sprouts</a></div></div></div> \n<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern" class="mw-redirect" title="Early modern">early modern</a> era, the food of England has historically been characterised by its simplicity of approach, honesty of flavour, and a reliance on the high quality of natural produce.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> This has resulted in a traditional cuisine which tended to avoid strong flavours, such as <a href="/wiki/Garlic" title="Garlic">garlic</a>, and also complex <a href="/wiki/Sauces" class="mw-redirect" title="Sauces">sauces</a> which were commonly associated with <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental</a> political affiliations.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Traditional meals have ancient origins, such as <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a>, popular today as the <a href="/wiki/Ploughman%27s_lunch" title="Ploughman&#39;s lunch">ploughman\'s lunch</a>, roasted and stewed meats, <a href="/wiki/Meat_pie" title="Meat pie">meat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Game_pie" title="Game pie">game</a> pies, and freshwater and saltwater fish. The 14th-century English cookbook, the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Forme_of_Cury" title="The Forme of Cury">Forme of Cury</a></i>, contains recipes for these, and dates from the royal court of <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II</a>.  Modern English cuisine is difficult to differentiate from <a href="/wiki/British_cuisine" title="British cuisine">British cuisine</a> as a whole. However, <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_dishes" title="List of English dishes">some dishes</a> are considered distinctively English.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Full_breakfast" title="Full breakfast">full English breakfast</a><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> typically comprises rashers of <a href="/wiki/Back_bacon" title="Back bacon">back bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (food)">fried and scrambled eggs</a>, pork <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausages</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_pudding" title="Black pudding">black pudding</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> grilled <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hash_browns" title="Hash browns">hash browns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baked_beans" title="Baked beans">baked beans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fried_bread" title="Fried bread">fried bread</a>, usually served with <a href="/wiki/Toast" title="Toast">toast</a> and a cup of <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Alternative breakfast dishes include <a href="/wiki/Boiled_egg" title="Boiled egg">boiled eggs</a> with toast <a href="/wiki/Soldiers_(food)" title="Soldiers (food)">soldiers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Craster_kipper" title="Craster kipper">kippers</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bubble_and_squeak" title="Bubble and squeak">bubble and squeak</a><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Roast_beef" title="Roast beef">Roast beef</a> is a food traditionally associated with the English; the link was made famous by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding" title="Henry Fielding">Henry Fielding</a>\'s patriotic <a href="/wiki/Ballad" title="Ballad">ballad</a> "<a href="/wiki/The_Roast_Beef_of_Old_England" title="The Roast Beef of Old England">The Roast Beef of Old England</a>", and <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/The_Gate_of_Calais" title="The Gate of Calais">painting of the same name</a>. Indeed, since the 18th century the phrase "les rosbifs" has been a popular French nickname for the English.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_pudding" title="Yorkshire pudding">Yorkshire puddings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horseradish#horseradish_sauce" title="Horseradish">horseradish sauce</a> or <a href="/wiki/English_mustard" class="mw-redirect" title="English mustard">English mustard</a> are usually served with roast beef.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Christmas_dinner#United_Kingdom" title="Christmas dinner">English Christmas dinner</a> traditionally consists of <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(bird)" title="Turkey (bird)">turkey</a>, which first appeared on the English Christmas dinner table in 1573, <a href="/wiki/Cranberry_sauce" title="Cranberry sauce">cranberry sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bread_sauce" title="Bread sauce">bread sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stuffing" title="Stuffing">stuffing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravy" title="Gravy">gravy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pigs_in_blankets" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigs in blankets">pigs in blankets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potatoes" class="mw-redirect" title="Potatoes">roast potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chestnut" title="Chestnut">chestnuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brussels_sprout" title="Brussels sprout">Brussels sprouts</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>.  It is sometimes accompanied by cooked <a href="/wiki/Gammon_(meat)" title="Gammon (meat)">gammon</a> and usually followed by <a href="/wiki/Christmas_pudding" title="Christmas pudding">Christmas pudding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mince_pie" title="Mince pie">mince pies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg/220px-Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg/330px-Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg/440px-Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3205" data-file-height="2900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sweeney_%26_Todd_meat_pie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family-sized meat pie made by Sweeney &amp; Todd of Reading</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg/220px-Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg/330px-Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg/440px-Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_adactio_164930387--Fish_and_chips.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fish and chips</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Sunday_roast" title="Sunday roast">Sunday roast</a> of roasted meat and potatoes with vegetables remains popular.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> One English commentator wrote that \xe2\x80\x9cWe have, throughout our history as a nation, had a weakness for meat in pastry which, while it is not unique, is a sort of hallmark of our taste.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Typical English main courses<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> include <a href="/wiki/Steak_and_kidney_pie" title="Steak and kidney pie">steak and kidney pie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicken_and_mushroom_pie" title="Chicken and mushroom pie">chicken and mushroom pie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacon_and_egg_pie" title="Bacon and egg pie">bacon and egg pie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shepherd%27s_pie" title="Shepherd&#39;s pie">shepherd\'s pie</a> (generally containing lamb), <a href="/wiki/Cottage_pie" class="mw-redirect" title="Cottage pie">cottage pie</a> (similar but made from beef), <a href="/wiki/Lancashire_hotpot" title="Lancashire hotpot">Lancashire hotpot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beef_Wellington" title="Beef Wellington">Beef Wellington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steak_and_kidney_pudding" title="Steak and kidney pudding">steak and kidney pudding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bedfordshire_clanger" title="Bedfordshire clanger">savoury bacon roll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish_and_chips" title="Fish and chips">fish and chips</a> (often served with <a href="/wiki/Mushy_peas" title="Mushy peas">mushy peas</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Fish_pie" title="Fish pie">fish pie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sausage_and_mash" class="mw-redirect" title="Sausage and mash">sausage and mash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toad_in_the_hole" title="Toad in the hole">toad in the hole</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Butchers sell artisanal sausages of high-quality, which are sometimes made from the meat of pedigree breeds such as <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire_Old_Spots" title="Gloucestershire Old Spots">Gloucester Old Spot pigs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_sausage" title="Cumberland sausage">Cumberland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_sausage" title="Lincolnshire sausage">Lincolnshire</a> sausages are popular regional varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Potatoes are served roasted, boiled, <a href="/wiki/Baked_potato" title="Baked potato">baked</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mashed_potato" title="Mashed potato">mashed</a>, and as <a href="/wiki/French_fries#United_Kingdom_and_Ireland" title="French fries">chips</a>; popular varieties in England including <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_potato" title="King Edward potato">King Edward</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey_Royal" title="Jersey Royal">Jersey Royal</a>, Charlotte and <a href="/wiki/Maris_Piper" title="Maris Piper">Maris Piper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dumpling#British_and_Irish_cuisine" title="Dumpling">Dumplings</a> made with flour, <a href="/wiki/Suet" title="Suet">suet</a> and seasonings are served with <a href="/wiki/Stew" title="Stew">stews</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> A wide range of fats and oils are used for cooking, including traditional <a href="/wiki/Lard" title="Lard">lard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The popularity of <a href="/wiki/Curry" title="Curry">curry</a> dishes, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chicken_Tikka_Masala" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicken Tikka Masala">Chicken Tikka Masala</a>, represents the ubiquity of Indian cultural influence throughout England. <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Indian_cuisine#dishes" title="Anglo-Indian cuisine">English-style chutneys</a> using orchard fruits originated from Indian chutneys. <a href="/wiki/Balti_(food)" title="Balti (food)">Balti</a> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Curry" title="Curry">curry</a> invented in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English savoury dishes and snacks include <a href="/wiki/Potted_shrimps" title="Potted shrimps">potted shrimps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cauliflower_cheese" title="Cauliflower cheese">cauliflower cheese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_rarebit" title="Welsh rarebit">Welsh rarebit</a>, Cornish <a href="/wiki/Pasty" title="Pasty">pasties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotch_egg" title="Scotch egg">Scotch eggs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sausage_roll" title="Sausage roll">sausage rolls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pork_pie" title="Pork pie">pork pies</a><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Bacon_sandwich" title="Bacon sandwich">bacon sandwich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sandwich" title="Sandwich">sandwich</a> was named after the <a href="/wiki/John_Montagu,_4th_Earl_of_Sandwich#The_sandwich" title="John Montagu, 4th Earl of Sandwich">Earl of Sandwich</a><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and is very common as a lunchtime and picnic item with a wide range of fillings.  <a href="/wiki/Worcestershire_sauce" title="Worcestershire sauce">Worcestershire sauce</a> is a distinctive English condiment.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> English desserts include <a href="/wiki/Bramley_apple" title="Bramley apple">Bramley</a> <a href="/wiki/Apple_pie" title="Apple pie">apple pie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bread_and_butter_pudding" title="Bread and butter pudding">bread and butter pudding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crumble" title="Crumble">crumble</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sticky_toffee_pudding" title="Sticky toffee pudding">sticky toffee pudding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treacle_tart" title="Treacle tart">treacle tart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treacle_sponge_pudding" title="Treacle sponge pudding">treacle sponge pudding</a> (made with <a href="/wiki/Golden_syrup" title="Golden syrup">golden syrup</a>), <a href="/wiki/Jam_roly-poly" title="Jam roly-poly">jam roly-poly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spotted_dick" title="Spotted dick">spotted dick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bakewell_tart" title="Bakewell tart">Bakewell tart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trifle" title="Trifle">trifle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eton_mess" title="Eton mess">Eton mess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Summer_pudding" title="Summer pudding">summer pudding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banoffee_pie" title="Banoffee pie">banoffee pie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Hot puddings are often served with <a href="/wiki/Custard" title="Custard">custard</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">Ice creams</a> are sometimes sold from <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream_van" title="Ice cream van">ice cream vans</a> which use distinctive chimes to attract customers<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northcote,_Battersea,_SW11_(4869265183).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Northcote%2C_Battersea%2C_SW11_%284869265183%29.jpg/220px-Northcote%2C_Battersea%2C_SW11_%284869265183%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Northcote%2C_Battersea%2C_SW11_%284869265183%29.jpg/330px-Northcote%2C_Battersea%2C_SW11_%284869265183%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Northcote%2C_Battersea%2C_SW11_%284869265183%29.jpg/440px-Northcote%2C_Battersea%2C_SW11_%284869265183%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3390" data-file-height="2260" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northcote,_Battersea,_SW11_(4869265183).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pub in <a href="/wiki/Battersea" title="Battersea">Battersea</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pubs</a> are widely accepted as an integral part of English culture</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg/220px-Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg" width="220" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg/330px-Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg/440px-Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2027" data-file-height="2195" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pimm%27s_O%27Clock.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Pimm\'s O\'Clock implies lazy, happy days sitting in the sun with nothing to do and no need to worry about time." Urban Dictionary</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg/220px-British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg/330px-British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg/440px-British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_dimpled_glass_pint_jug_with_ale.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pint of real ale</div></div></div>\n<p>Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Tea_(meal)#Tea_as_the_evening_meal" title="Tea (meal)">High Tea</a> would be had as a full evening meal, whereas <a href="/wiki/Tea_(meal)#Afternoon_Tea" title="Tea (meal)">Afternoon Tea</a> was a lighter meal taken earlier in the afternoon only by some classes of society. A <a href="/wiki/Cream_tea" title="Cream tea">cream tea</a> includes <a href="/wiki/Scone" title="Scone">scones</a> served with jam and <a href="/wiki/Clotted_cream" title="Clotted cream">clotted cream</a> from <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>, sometimes accompanied by dainty <a href="/wiki/Tea_sandwich" title="Tea sandwich">finger sandwiches</a>, with fillings such as <a href="/wiki/Cucumber_sandwich" title="Cucumber sandwich">cucumber</a><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>, and a selection of cakes such as <a href="/wiki/Victoria_sponge" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria sponge">Victoria sponge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Other items served for afternoon tea<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> include <a href="/wiki/Teacake" title="Teacake">teacakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crumpet" title="Crumpet">crumpets</a> and pikelets,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muffin_(English)" class="mw-redirect" title="Muffin (English)">English muffins</a> (a form of rounded, yeast-leavened bread, also used as the base of <a href="/wiki/Eggs_Benedict" title="Eggs Benedict">eggs Benedict</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Lardy_cake" title="Lardy cake">lardy cakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> English cakes include <a href="/wiki/Cupcake" title="Cupcake">fairy cakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eccles_cake" title="Eccles cake">Eccles cake</a> (from Eccles, Greater Manchester), <a href="/wiki/Battenberg_cake" title="Battenberg cake">Battenberg cake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simnel_cake" title="Simnel cake">Simnel cake</a> (the latter being associated with <a href="/wiki/Mothering_Sunday" title="Mothering Sunday">Mothering Sunday</a> and <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> whilst there are several types of fruited bun such as <a href="/wiki/Currant_bun" title="Currant bun">currant buns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_bun" title="Chelsea bun">Chelsea buns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hot_cross_bun" title="Hot cross bun">hot cross buns</a> (the latter marking <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday" title="Good Friday">Good Friday</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pancake#England" title="Pancake">English Pancakes</a> are popular on <a href="/wiki/Shrove_Tuesday" title="Shrove Tuesday">Shrove Tuesday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">Tea</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_beer" class="mw-redirect" title="English beer">beer</a> are typical and rather iconic drinks in England.  A cup of tea is often accompanied with a <a href="/wiki/Biscuit#Commonwealth_of_Nations_and_Europe" title="Biscuit">biscuit</a> or piece of cake.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Tea_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Tea in the United Kingdom">tea drinking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teahouse" title="Teahouse">tearooms</a> have declined since the rise of global chains of <a href="/wiki/Coffeehouse#United_Kingdom" title="Coffeehouse">coffee shops</a> in the 1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> beer has seen a resurgence, particularly due to increased interest in <a href="/wiki/Cask_ale#Real_ale" title="Cask ale">real ale</a> and the marketing success of the term "craft beer".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cider" title="Cider">Cider</a> is produced in the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Beer and cider are generally served <a href="/wiki/Draught_beer" title="Draught beer">draught</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pubs</a> by the pint or half-pint, either in a straight glass or a dimpled glass tankard (known as a <a href="/wiki/Jug#beer_jug" title="Jug">jug</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gin" title="Gin">Gin</a> has been popular in England since the late 17th century and is often served with <a href="/wiki/Gin_and_tonic" title="Gin and tonic">tonic water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pimms" class="mw-redirect" title="Pimms">Pimm\'s No. 1 Cup</a>, a gin-based drink containing a mixture of herbs and liquors, is popular for making <a href="/wiki/Punch_(drink)" title="Punch (drink)">punch</a> for summer social events.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> More recently, the south of England has seen the reintroduction of vineyards making <a href="/wiki/Wine_from_the_United_Kingdom#English_wine" title="Wine from the United Kingdom">English wine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although English cheesemaking was restricted by <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom#Post-Second_World_War" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">wartime rationing</a>, there are now over 750 different cheeses<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> including traditional (generally hard) regional cheeses such as:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cheddar_cheese" title="Cheddar cheese">Cheddar cheese</a></li>\n<li>Blue and White <a href="/wiki/Stilton_cheese" title="Stilton cheese">Stilton cheese</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Wensleydale_cheese" title="Wensleydale cheese">Wensleydale cheese</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lancashire_cheese" title="Lancashire cheese">Lancashire cheese</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dorset_Blue_Vinney_cheese" class="mw-redirect" title="Dorset Blue Vinney cheese">Dorset Blue Vinney cheese</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cheshire_cheese" title="Cheshire cheese">Cheshire cheese</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Double_Gloucester_cheese" class="mw-redirect" title="Double Gloucester cheese">Double Gloucester cheese</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Red_Leicester" title="Red Leicester">Red Leicester</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Shropshire_Blue" title="Shropshire Blue">Shropshire Blue</a> cheese</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Swaledale_cheese" title="Swaledale cheese">Swaledale cheese</a></li></ul>\n<p>Recent decades have seen the production of semi-soft or soft cheeses, such as <a href="/wiki/Stinking_Bishop_cheese" title="Stinking Bishop cheese">Stinking Bishop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Yarg" title="Cornish Yarg">Cornish Yarg</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wigmore_cheese&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wigmore cheese (page does not exist)">Wigmore cheese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brie#International_bries" title="Brie">Brie</a>.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_cheeses" title="List of English cheeses">List of English cheeses</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Folklore">Folklore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Folklore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_folklore" title="English folklore">English folklore</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png" class="image"><img alt="Drawing depicting Robin Hood, wearing Lincoln green clothing, and Sir Guy of Gisbourne, wearing brown furs, in a forest preparing to shoot with bows and arrows." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png/170px-Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png" width="170" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png/255px-Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png 2x" data-file-width="317" data-file-height="466" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robin_shoots_with_sir_Guy_by_Louis_Rhead_1912.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> illustrated in 1912 wearing <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_green" title="Lincoln green">Lincoln green</a></div></div></div>\n<p>English folklore developed over many centuries. Some of the characters and stories are present all over England, but most belong to specific regions. Common folkloric beings include <a href="/wiki/Pixie" title="Pixie">pixies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giant_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Giant (mythology)">giants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elf" title="Elf">elves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bogeymen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bogeymen">bogeymen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troll" title="Troll">trolls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goblin" title="Goblin">goblins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_(mythology)" title="Dwarf (mythology)">dwarves</a>. While many legends and folk-customs are thought to be ancient, for instance the tales featuring <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Angel" title="Offa of Angel">Offa of Angel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wayland_the_Smith" title="Wayland the Smith">Wayland the Smith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-keary_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keary-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> others date from after the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a>; <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Merry_Men" title="Merry Men">Merry Men</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Forest" title="Sherwood Forest">Sherwood</a> and their battles with the <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_of_Nottingham" title="Sheriff of Nottingham">Sheriff of Nottingham</a> being, perhaps, the best known.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> tales originated from Brythonic traditions, notably the <a href="/wiki/Arthurian_legend" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthurian legend">Arthurian legend</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-woodbbc_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-woodbbc-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Deriving from Welsh sources; <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Excalibur" title="Excalibur">Excalibur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merlin" title="Merlin">Merlin</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Jersey</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Wace" title="Wace">Wace</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_the_Round_Table" title="Knights of the Round Table">Knights of the Round Table</a>. These stories are most centrally brought together within <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></i>. Another early figure from <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historic)">British tradition</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_Cole" class="mw-redirect" title="King Cole">King Cole</a>, may have been based on a real figure from Sub-Roman Britain. Many of the tales and <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-history" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudo-history">pseudo-histories</a> make up part of the wider <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Matter of Britain</a>, a collection of shared British folklore.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg/220px-Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg/330px-Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg/440px-Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cotswold_Morris_handkerchiefs_20040501.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Morris dancing is one of the more visible English folk traditions, with many differing regional variations.</div></div></div>\n<p>Some folk figures are based on semi or actual historical people whose story has been passed down centuries; <a href="/wiki/Lady_Godiva" title="Lady Godiva">Lady Godiva</a> for instance was said to have ridden naked on horseback through <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hereward_the_Wake" title="Hereward the Wake">Hereward the Wake</a> was a heroic English figure resisting the Norman invasion, <a href="/wiki/Herne_the_Hunter" title="Herne the Hunter">Herne the Hunter</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrian</a> ghost associated with <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Berkshire" title="Windsor, Berkshire">Windsor</a> Forest and <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Great_Park" title="Windsor Great Park">Great Park</a> (whose tale bears the common European folkloric motif of the <a href="/wiki/Wild_Hunt" title="Wild Hunt">Wild Hunt</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Mother_Shipton" title="Mother Shipton">Mother Shipton</a> is the archetypal witch.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The chivalrous bandit, such as <a href="/wiki/Dick_Turpin" title="Dick Turpin">Dick Turpin</a>, is a recurring character. There are various national and regional folk activities, participated in to this day, such as <a href="/wiki/Morris_dancing" class="mw-redirect" title="Morris dancing">Morris dancing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maypole_dance" class="mw-redirect" title="Maypole dance">Maypole dancing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapper_sword" title="Rapper sword">Rapper sword</a> in the North East, <a href="/wiki/Long_Sword_dance" title="Long Sword dance">Long Sword dance</a> in Yorkshire, <a href="/wiki/Mummers_Play" class="mw-redirect" title="Mummers Play">Mummers Plays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bottle-kicking" title="Bottle-kicking">bottle-kicking</a> in Leicestershire, and <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_Hill_Cheese-Rolling_and_Wake" title="Cooper&#39;s Hill Cheese-Rolling and Wake">cheese-rolling</a> at <a href="/wiki/Brockworth,_Gloucestershire" title="Brockworth, Gloucestershire">Cooper\'s Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> There is no official national costume, but a few are well established such as the <a href="/wiki/Pearly_Kings_and_Queens" title="Pearly Kings and Queens">Pearly Kings and Queens</a> associated with cockneys, the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Guard" title="Queen&#39;s Guard">Royal Guard</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Morris_dance" title="Morris dance">Morris costume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beefeater" class="mw-redirect" title="Beefeater">Beefeaters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The utopian vision of a traditional England is sometimes referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Merry_England" title="Merry England">Merry England</a></i>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bbeard_Sword.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Bbeard_Sword.jpg/170px-Bbeard_Sword.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Bbeard_Sword.jpg/255px-Bbeard_Sword.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Bbeard_Sword.jpg/340px-Bbeard_Sword.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2144" data-file-height="2716" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bbeard_Sword.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving of the English pirate <a href="/wiki/Blackbeard" title="Blackbeard">Blackbeard</a> from the 1724 book <i><a href="/wiki/A_General_History_of_the_Pyrates" title="A General History of the Pyrates">A General History of the Pyrates</a></i>. The book is the prime source for many famous pirates of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Piracy" title="Golden Age of Piracy">Golden Age of Piracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pirates_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pirates-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Published in 1724, <i><a href="/wiki/A_General_History_of_the_Pyrates" title="A General History of the Pyrates">A General History of the Pyrates</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Johnson_(pirate_biographer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Johnson (pirate biographer)">Captain Charles Johnson</a> provided the standard account of the lives of many pirates in the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Piracy" title="Golden Age of Piracy">Golden Age of Piracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pirates_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pirates-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Many famous English pirates from the Golden Age hailed from the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a> in the south west coast of England\xe2\x80\x94the stereotypical <a href="/wiki/West_Country_English#Social_stigma_and_future_of_West_Country_dialect" title="West Country English">West Country</a> "<a href="/wiki/Pirates_in_popular_culture#Appearance_and_mannerisms_of_Caribbean_pirates" title="Pirates in popular culture">pirate accent</a>" was popularised by West Country native <a href="/wiki/Robert_Newton" title="Robert Newton">Robert Newton</a>\'s portrayal of <a href="/wiki/Long_John_Silver" title="Long John Silver">Long John Silver</a> in film.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The concept of "<a href="/wiki/Walking_the_plank" title="Walking the plank">walking the plank</a>" was popularised by <a href="/wiki/J._M._Barrie" title="J. M. Barrie">J. M. Barrie</a>\'s novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_and_Wendy" title="Peter and Wendy">Peter Pan</a></i>, where <a href="/wiki/Captain_Hook" title="Captain Hook">Captain Hook</a>\'s pirates helped define the archetype.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Davy_Jones%27_Locker" title="Davy Jones&#39; Locker">Davy Jones\' Locker</a> where sailors or ship\'s remains are consigned to the bottom of the sea is first recorded by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a> in 1726.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Johnson\'s 1724 book gave a mythical status to famous English pirates such as <a href="/wiki/Blackbeard" title="Blackbeard">Blackbeard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calico_Jack" title="Calico Jack">Calico Jack</a>. Jack is known for his <a href="/wiki/Jolly_Roger" title="Jolly Roger">Jolly Roger</a> flag design, a skull with crossed swords.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gremlin" title="Gremlin">Gremlin</a> is part of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Air force">Royal Air force</a> folklore dating from the 1920s, with gremlin being RAF slang for a mischievous creature that sabotages aircraft, meddling in the plane\'s equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Legendary figures from 19th century London whose tales have been romanticised include <a href="/wiki/Sweeney_Todd" title="Sweeney Todd">Sweeney Todd</a>, the murderous barber of <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Street" title="Fleet Street">Fleet Street</a>, and serial killer <a href="/wiki/Jack_the_Ripper" title="Jack the Ripper">Jack the Ripper</a>. On 5 November, people in England make bonfires, set off <a href="/wiki/Firework" class="mw-redirect" title="Firework">fireworks</a> and eat <a href="/wiki/Toffee_apple" class="mw-redirect" title="Toffee apple">toffee apples</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guy_Fawkes_Night" title="Guy Fawkes Night">commemoration</a> of the foiling of  <a href="/wiki/Guy_Fawkes" title="Guy Fawkes">Guy Fawkes</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_Plot" title="Gunpowder Plot">Gunpowder Plot</a>, which became an annual event after <i>The Thanksgiving Act</i> of 1606 was passed.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guy_Fawkes_mask" title="Guy Fawkes mask">Guy Fawkes mask</a> is an emblem for <a href="/wiki/Anti-establishment" title="Anti-establishment">anti-establishment</a> protest groups.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language_in_England" title="English language in England">English language in England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglospeak.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Anglospeak.svg/250px-Anglospeak.svg.png" width="250" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Anglospeak.svg/375px-Anglospeak.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Anglospeak.svg/500px-Anglospeak.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglospeak.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries where <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> has official status or is widely spoken</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English people</a> traditionally speak the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>, a member of the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a>. The modern English language is now known as the potential Norman speakings but has now \nevolved from <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle English language">Middle English</a> (the form of language in use by the English people from the 12th to the 15th century); Middle English was influenced lexically by Norman-French, Old French and Latin. In the Middle English period Latin was the language of administration and the nobility spoke Norman French. Middle English was itself derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a> of the Anglo-Saxon period; in the Northern and Eastern parts of England the language of Danish settlers had influenced the language, a fact still evident in Northern English dialects. During its history Modern English has drawn extensively from the vocabulary other languages. e.g. French, Latin, Greek, Dutch and to a lesser extent from many others.\n</p><p>There were once many different dialects of modern English in England - which were recorded in projects such as the <i><a href="/wiki/English_Dialect_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="English Dialect Dictionary">English Dialect Dictionary</a></i> (late 19th century) and the <a href="/wiki/Survey_of_English_Dialects" title="Survey of English Dialects">Survey of English Dialects</a> (mid 20th century), but many of these have passed out of common usage as <a href="/wiki/Standard_English" title="Standard English">Standard English</a> has become more widespread through education, the media and socio-economic pressures.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic language</a>, is one of three existing Brythonic languages; its usage has been revived in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>. Historically, another Brythonic Celtic language, <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric</a>, was spoken in <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a>, but it died out in the 11th century although traces of it can still be found in the Cumbrian dialect. <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a> began in the late 15th century with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> to London and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a>. Through the worldwide influence of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, English spread around the world from the 17th to mid-20th centuries. Through newspapers, books, the telegraph, the telephone, phonograph records, radio, satellite television, broadcasters (such as the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>) and the Internet, as well as the post WWII emergence of the United States as a global superpower, Modern English has become the <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">international language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sports" class="mw-redirect" title="Sports">sports</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy" title="Diplomacy">diplomacy</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surnames">Surnames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg/220px-Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg/330px-Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg/440px-Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_courts_of_justice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Courts_of_Justice" title="Royal Courts of Justice">Royal Courts of Justice</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Strand,_London" title="Strand, London">Strand, London</a> is the seat of the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court of Justice</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Appeal_of_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Court of Appeal of England and Wales">Court of Appeal</a></div></div></div>\n<p>English law is the <a href="/wiki/Legal_systems_of_the_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal systems of the world">legal system</a> of <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, it has been exported across the world: it is the basis of <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The 18th century English jurist, judge and politician Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Blackstone" title="William Blackstone">William Blackstone</a> is best known for his seminal work, <i><a href="/wiki/Commentaries_on_the_Laws_of_England" title="Commentaries on the Laws of England">Commentaries on the Laws of England</a></i>, containing his formulation: "It is better that ten guilty persons escape than that one innocent suffer", a principle that government and the courts must err on the side of innocence, which has remained constant.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Garrow" title="William Garrow">William Garrow</a> ushered in the <a href="/wiki/Adversarial_system" title="Adversarial system">adversarial court system</a> in common law. He coined the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">presumed innocent until proven guilty</a>", insisting that defendants\' accusers and their evidence be thoroughly tested in court.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Major constitutional documents include: <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a> (foundation of the "great writ" <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">Habeas corpus</a></i> \xe2\x80\x94 safeguarding individual freedom against arbitrary state action), the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a> (one provision granting <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a> in Parliament), <a href="/wiki/Petition_of_Right" title="Petition of Right">Petition of Right</a>, <a href="/wiki/Habeas_Corpus_Act_1679" title="Habeas Corpus Act 1679">Habeas Corpus Act 1679</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Acts_1911_and_1949" title="Parliament Acts 1911 and 1949">Parliament Acts 1911 and 1949</a>. The jurist <a href="/wiki/Albert_Venn_Dicey" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Venn Dicey">Albert Venn Dicey</a> wrote that the British Habeas Corpus Acts "declare no principle and define no rights, but they are for practical purposes worth a hundred constitutional articles guaranteeing individual liberty".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> A strong advocate of the "unwritten constitution", Dicey stated English rights were embedded in English common law, and "the institutions and manners of the nation".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_literature" title="English literature">English literature</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Hogarth_017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/William_Hogarth_017.jpg/220px-William_Hogarth_017.jpg" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/William_Hogarth_017.jpg/330px-William_Hogarth_017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/William_Hogarth_017.jpg/440px-William_Hogarth_017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2038" data-file-height="1658" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Hogarth_017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a>\'s depiction of a scene from Shakespeare\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest" title="The Tempest">The Tempest</a></i> is an example of how English literature influenced English painting in the 18th century</div></div></div>\n<p>English literature begins with <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon literature">Anglo-Saxon literature</a>, which was written in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and produced such heroic epic works as <a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a> and the fragmentary <a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Maldon" title="The Battle of Maldon">The Battle of Maldon</a>, the sombre and introspective <a href="/wiki/The_Seafarer_(poem)" title="The Seafarer (poem)">The Seafarer</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Wanderer_(poem)" title="The Wanderer (poem)">The Wanderer</a> and the pious <a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Rood" title="Dream of the Rood">Dream of the Rood</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Order_of_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="The Order of the World (page does not exist)">The Order of the World</a>. For many years, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> were the preferred literary languages of England, but in the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval period">medieval period</a> there was a flourishing of literature in <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>; <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> is the most famous writer of this period. The <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan era</a> is sometimes described as the golden age of English literature, as numerous great poets were writing in English, and the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan theatre">Elizabethan theatre</a> produced <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, often considered the English national poet.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woking_tripod.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Woking_tripod.JPG/170px-Woking_tripod.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Woking_tripod.JPG/255px-Woking_tripod.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Woking_tripod.JPG/340px-Woking_tripod.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Woking_tripod.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of a <a href="/wiki/Tripod_(The_War_of_the_Worlds)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tripod (The War of the Worlds)">tripod</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i> in Woking, hometown of science fiction author <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>. The book is a seminal depiction of a conflict between mankind and an <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrials_in_fiction" title="Extraterrestrials in fiction">extraterrestrial</a> race.</div></div></div>\n<p>Due to the expansion of English into a <a href="/wiki/World_language" title="World language">world language</a> during the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, literature is now written in English across the world. Writers often associated with England or for expressing Englishness include Shakespeare (who produced two <a href="/wiki/Tetralogies" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetralogies">tetralogies</a> of <a href="/wiki/History_play" class="mw-redirect" title="History play">history plays</a> about the English kings), <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Bennett" title="Arnold Bennett">Arnold Bennett</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Brooke" title="Rupert Brooke">Rupert Brooke</a> (whose poem "Grantchester" is often considered quintessentially English). Other writers are associated with specific regions of England; these include <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> (<a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>), <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" title="Thomas Hardy">Thomas Hardy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy%27s_Wessex" title="Thomas Hardy&#39;s Wessex">Wessex</a>), <a href="/wiki/A._E._Housman" title="A. E. Housman">A. E. Housman</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Poets" title="Lake Poets">Lake Poets</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Lake_District" title="Lake District">Lake District</a>).\n</p><p>In the lighter vein, <a href="/wiki/Agatha_Christie" title="Agatha Christie">Agatha Christie</a>\'s mystery novels are outsold only by Shakespeare and The Bible. Described as "perhaps the 20th century\'s best chronicler of English culture", the non-fiction works of <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road_to_Wigan_Pier" title="The Road to Wigan Pier">The Road to Wigan Pier</a></i> (1937), documenting his experience of working class life in the north of England.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Orwell\'s eleven rules for making tea appear in his essay "<a href="/wiki/A_Nice_Cup_of_Tea" title="A Nice Cup of Tea">A Nice Cup of Tea</a>", which was published in the <i>London Evening Standard</i> on 12 January 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2003 the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> carried out a UK survey entitled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Read" title="The Big Read">The Big Read</a></i> in order to find the "nation\'s best-loved novel" of all time, with works by English novelists <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Pullman" title="Philip Pullman">Philip Pullman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams">Douglas Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._K._Rowling" title="J. K. Rowling">J. K. Rowling</a> making up the top five on the list.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Folk_music_of_England" title="Folk music of England">Folk music of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Music of the United Kingdom">Music of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_Elgar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Edward_Elgar.jpg/200px-Edward_Elgar.jpg" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Edward_Elgar.jpg/300px-Edward_Elgar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Edward_Elgar.jpg/400px-Edward_Elgar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="697" data-file-height="698" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_Elgar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Edward Elgar</a> is one of England\'s most celebrated classical composers.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_Mural.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/David_Bowie_Mural.jpg/200px-David_Bowie_Mural.jpg" width="200" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/David_Bowie_Mural.jpg/300px-David_Bowie_Mural.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/David_Bowie_Mural.jpg/400px-David_Bowie_Mural.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1872" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_Mural.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a>\'s mural in his hometown of Brixton features his character <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_Sane" title="Aladdin Sane">Aladdin Sane</a></i>. It became a shrine to Bowie after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>England has a long and rich musical history. The United Kingdom has, like most European countries, undergone a roots revival in the last half of the 20th century. English music has been an instrumental and leading part of this phenomenon, which peaked at the end of the 1960s and into the 1970s.\n</p><p>The achievements of the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_church_music" title="Anglican church music">Anglican choral tradition</a> following on from 16th-century composers such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Tallis" title="Thomas Tallis">Thomas Tallis</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Taverner" title="John Taverner">John Taverner</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Byrd" title="William Byrd">William Byrd</a> have tended to overshadow instrumental composition.
