The semi-operatic innovations of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Henry Purcell</a> did not lead to a native operatic tradition, but <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a> found important royal patrons and enthusiastic public support in England.  One of Handel\'s four <a href="/wiki/Coronation_Anthems_(Handel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation Anthems (Handel)">Coronation Anthems</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Zadok_the_Priest" title="Zadok the Priest">Zadok the Priest</a></i> (1727), composed for the coronation of <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George II</a>, has been performed at every subsequent <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_monarch" title="Coronation of the British monarch">British coronation</a>, traditionally during the sovereign\'s anointing. The rapturous receptions afforded by audiences to visiting musical celebrities such as <a href="/wiki/Haydn" class="mw-redirect" title="Haydn">Haydn</a> often contrasted with the lack of recognition for home-grown talent. However, the emergence of figures such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Edward Elgar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sullivan" title="Arthur Sullivan">Arthur Sullivan</a> in the 19th century showed a new vitality in English music. In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Britten" title="Benjamin Britten">Benjamin Britten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tippett" title="Michael Tippett">Michael Tippett</a> emerged as internationally recognised <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> composers, and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a> and others collected English folk tunes and adapted them to the concert hall. <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Sharp" title="Cecil Sharp">Cecil Sharp</a> was a leading figure in the English folk revival.  <a href="/wiki/The_Proms" title="The Proms">The Proms</a>, an annual summer season of daily classical music concerts, is a significant event in British musical life.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/The_Proms#Last_Night_of_the_Proms" title="The Proms">Last Night of the Proms</a> features patriotic music.\n</p><p>Finally, a new trend emerged from <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> in 1962. <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> became the most popular musicians of their time, and in the composing duo of <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>, popularized the concept of the self-contained music act. Before the Beatles, very few popular singers composed the tunes they performed.  The "Fab Four" opened the doors for other acts from England such as <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">The Rolling Stones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">The Kinks</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Hollies" title="The Hollies">The Hollies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(band)" title="Genesis (band)">Genesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dire_Straits" title="Dire Straits">Dire Straits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Police" title="The Police">The Police</a>  to the globe.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sex_Pistols" title="Sex Pistols">Sex Pistols</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Clash" title="The Clash">The Clash</a> were pioneers of punk rock. Some of England\'s leading contemporary artists include <a href="/wiki/George_Michael" title="George Michael">George Michael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sting_(musician)" title="Sting (musician)">Sting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seal_(musician)" title="Seal (musician)">Seal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rod_Stewart" title="Rod Stewart">Rod Stewart</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Smiths" title="The Smiths">The Smiths</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Stone_Roses" title="The Stone Roses">The Stone Roses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oasis_(band)" title="Oasis (band)">Oasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blur_(band)" title="Blur (band)">Blur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiohead" title="Radiohead">Radiohead</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Cure" title="The Cure">The Cure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Depeche_Mode" title="Depeche Mode">Depeche Mode</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coldplay" title="Coldplay">Coldplay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Def_Leppard" title="Def Leppard">Def Leppard</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Muse_(band)" title="Muse (band)">Muse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Monkeys" title="Arctic Monkeys">Arctic Monkeys</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Amy_Winehouse" title="Amy Winehouse">Amy Winehouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adele" title="Adele">Adele</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ed_Sheeran" title="Ed Sheeran">Ed Sheeran</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cinema of the United Kingdom">Cinema of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:217px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hitchcock,_Alfred_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg/215px-Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg" width="215" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg/323px-Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg/430px-Hitchcock%2C_Alfred_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="802" data-file-height="890" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hitchcock,_Alfred_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a> is often regarded as the greatest British filmmaker.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> He was described as "a straightforward middle-class Englishman who just happened to be an artistic genius."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>England (and the UK as a whole) has had a considerable influence on the history of the cinema, producing some of the greatest actors, directors and motion pictures of all time, including <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Lean" title="David Lean">David Lean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vivien_Leigh" title="Vivien Leigh">Vivien Leigh</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Gielgud" title="John Gielgud">John Gielgud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sellers" title="Peter Sellers">Peter Sellers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julie_Andrews" title="Julie Andrews">Julie Andrews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Caine" title="Michael Caine">Michael Caine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gary_Oldman" title="Gary Oldman">Gary Oldman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helen_Mirren" title="Helen Mirren">Helen Mirren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kate_Winslet" title="Kate Winslet">Kate Winslet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Day-Lewis" title="Daniel Day-Lewis">Daniel Day-Lewis</a>. Hitchcock and Lean are among the most critically acclaimed of all-time.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Hitchcock\'s first thriller, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lodger:_A_Story_of_the_London_Fog" title="The Lodger: A Story of the London Fog">The Lodger: A Story of the London Fog</a></i> (1926), helped shape the <a href="/wiki/Thriller_(genre)" title="Thriller (genre)">thriller</a> genre in film, while his 1929 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Blackmail_(1929_film)" title="Blackmail (1929 film)">Blackmail</a></i>, is often regarded as the first British <a href="/wiki/Sound_film#Transition:_Europe" title="Sound film">sound</a> feature film.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Major film studios in England include <a href="/wiki/Pinewood_Studios" title="Pinewood Studios">Pinewood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elstree_Studios" title="Elstree Studios">Elstree</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_Studios" title="Shepperton Studios">Shepperton</a>. Some of the most commercially successful films of all time have been produced in England, including two of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_film_series" class="mw-redirect" title="List of highest-grossing film series">highest-grossing film franchises</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_(film_series)" title="Harry Potter (film series)">Harry Potter</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/James_Bond_(film_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Bond (film series)">James Bond</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ealing_Studios" title="Ealing Studios">Ealing Studios</a> in London has a claim to being the oldest continuously working film studio in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Famous for recording many motion picture <a href="/wiki/Film_scores" class="mw-redirect" title="Film scores">film scores</a>, the <a href="/wiki/London_Symphony_Orchestra" title="London Symphony Orchestra">London Symphony Orchestra</a> first performed film music in 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dracula_1958_c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Dracula_1958_c.jpg/190px-Dracula_1958_c.jpg" width="190" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Dracula_1958_c.jpg/285px-Dracula_1958_c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Dracula_1958_c.jpg/380px-Dracula_1958_c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="866" data-file-height="473" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dracula_1958_c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Lee" title="Christopher Lee">Christopher Lee</a> (as <a href="/wiki/Count_Dracula" title="Count Dracula">Dracula</a> in 1958) starred in numerous British horror films produced by <a href="/wiki/Hammer_Film_Productions" title="Hammer Film Productions">Hammer</a>. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Powell" title="Michael Powell">Michael Powell</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Peeping_Tom_(film)" title="Peeping Tom (film)">Peeping Tom</a></i> (1960) was the first "<a href="/wiki/Slasher_film" title="Slasher film">slasher</a>" film.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/BFI_Top_100_British_films" title="BFI Top 100 British films">BFI Top 100 British films</a> includes <i><a href="/wiki/Monty_Python%27s_Life_of_Brian" title="Monty Python&#39;s Life of Brian">Monty Python\'s Life of Brian</a></i> (1979), a film regularly voted the funniest of all time by the UK public.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> English producers are also active in <a href="/wiki/International_co-productions" class="mw-redirect" title="International co-productions">international co-productions</a> and English actors, directors and crew feature regularly in Hollywood films. <a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a> was among a group of English filmmakers, including <a href="/wiki/Tony_Scott" title="Tony Scott">Tony Scott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Parker" title="Alan Parker">Alan Parker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Hudson" title="Hugh Hudson">Hugh Hudson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Lyne" title="Adrian Lyne">Adrian Lyne</a>, who emerged from making 1970s UK television commercials.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The UK film council ranked <a href="/wiki/David_Yates" title="David Yates">David Yates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Nolan" title="Christopher Nolan">Christopher Nolan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Newell_(director)" title="Mike Newell (director)">Mike Newell</a>, Ridley Scott and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Greengrass" title="Paul Greengrass">Paul Greengrass</a> the five most commercially successful English directors since 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Other contemporary  directors from England include <a href="/wiki/Sam_Mendes" title="Sam Mendes">Sam Mendes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guy_Ritchie" title="Guy Ritchie">Guy Ritchie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_McQueen_(director)" title="Steve McQueen (director)">Steve McQueen</a>. Current actors include <a href="/wiki/Tom_Hardy" title="Tom Hardy">Tom Hardy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Craig" title="Daniel Craig">Daniel Craig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Cumberbatch" title="Benedict Cumberbatch">Benedict Cumberbatch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emma_Watson" title="Emma Watson">Emma Watson</a>. Acclaimed for his motion capture work, <a href="/wiki/Andy_Serkis" title="Andy Serkis">Andy Serkis</a> opened <a href="/wiki/The_Imaginarium_Studios" class="mw-redirect" title="The Imaginarium Studios">The Imaginarium Studios</a> in London in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The visual effects company <a href="/wiki/Framestore" title="Framestore">Framestore</a> in London has produced some of the most critically acclaimed special effects in modern film.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Many successful Hollywood films have been based on English people, <a href="/wiki/British_literature" title="British literature">stories</a> or events. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios_films" title="List of Walt Disney Animation Studios films">\'English Cycle\'</a> of Disney animated films include <i><a href="/wiki/Alice_in_Wonderland" class="mw-redirect" title="Alice in Wonderland">Alice in Wonderland</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jungle_Book" title="The Jungle Book">The Jungle Book</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Winnie_the_Pooh" class="mw-redirect" title="Winnie the Pooh">Winnie the Pooh</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Performing_arts">Performing arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Performing arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Albert_Hall,_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Royal_Albert_Hall%2C_London.jpg/220px-Royal_Albert_Hall%2C_London.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Royal_Albert_Hall%2C_London.jpg/330px-Royal_Albert_Hall%2C_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Royal_Albert_Hall%2C_London.jpg/440px-Royal_Albert_Hall%2C_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6450" data-file-height="4150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Albert_Hall,_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Proms" title="The Proms">The Proms</a> are held annually at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Hall" title="Royal Albert Hall">Royal Albert Hall</a> during the summer</div></div></div>\n<p>Large outdoor <a href="/wiki/List_of_music_festivals_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of music festivals in the United Kingdom">music festivals</a> in the summer and autumn are popular, such as <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Festival" title="Glastonbury Festival">Glastonbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/V_Festival" title="V Festival">V Festival</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reading_and_Leeds_Festivals" title="Reading and Leeds Festivals">Reading and Leeds Festivals</a>. England was at the forefront of the illegal, free <a href="/wiki/Rave" title="Rave">rave</a> movement from the late 1980s, which led to pan-European culture of <a href="/wiki/Teknival" title="Teknival">teknivals</a> mirrored on the UK free festival movement and associated travelling lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The most prominent <a href="/wiki/Opera_house" title="Opera house">opera house</a> in England is the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Opera_House" title="Royal Opera House">Royal Opera House</a> at <a href="/wiki/Covent_Garden" title="Covent Garden">Covent Garden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-foreman_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foreman-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Proms" title="The Proms">The Proms</a>, a season of <a href="/wiki/Orchestral" class="mw-redirect" title="Orchestral">orchestral</a> classical music concerts held at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Hall" title="Royal Albert Hall">Royal Albert Hall</a>, is a major cultural event held annually.<sup id="cite_ref-foreman_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foreman-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Ballet" title="The Royal Ballet">The Royal Ballet</a> is one of the world\'s foremost classical ballet companies, its reputation built on two prominent figures of 20th century dance, <i><a href="/wiki/Prima_ballerina" class="mw-redirect" title="Prima ballerina">prima ballerina</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Margot_Fonteyn" title="Margot Fonteyn">Margot Fonteyn</a> and choreographer <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Ashton" title="Frederick Ashton">Frederick Ashton</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG/220px-Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG/330px-Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG/440px-Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2349" data-file-height="2102" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Swanage_Punch_%26_Judy.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Punch_and_Judy" title="Punch and Judy">Punch and Judy</a> booth at <a href="/wiki/Swanage" title="Swanage">Swanage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorset" title="Dorset">Dorset</a>. The anarchic Mr. Punch has been an influential figure in <a href="/wiki/British_comedy" title="British comedy">British comedy</a> and political cartoons.</div></div></div>\n<p>A staple of British seaside culture, the quarrelsome couple <a href="/wiki/Punch_and_Judy" title="Punch and Judy">Punch and Judy</a> made their first recorded appearance in Covent Garden, London in 1662.<sup id="cite_ref-British_seaside_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_seaside-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The various episodes of Punch and Judy are performed in the spirit of outrageous comedy \xe2\x80\x94 often provoking shocked laughter \xe2\x80\x94 and are dominated by the anarchic clowning of Mr. Punch.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Regarded as British cultural icons, they appeared at a significant period in British history, with Glyn Edwards stating: "[Pulcinella] went down particularly well with Restoration British audiences, fun-starved after years of <a href="/wiki/Puritanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a>. We soon changed Punch\'s name, transformed him from a marionette to a hand puppet, and he became, really, a spirit of Britain - a subversive maverick who defies authority, a kind of puppet equivalent to our political cartoons."<sup id="cite_ref-British_seaside_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_seaside-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg/210px-Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg" width="210" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg/315px-Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg/420px-Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="626" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astley%27s_Amphitheatre_Microcosm_edited.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre" title="Astley&#39;s Amphitheatre">Astley\'s Amphitheatre</a>, London circa 1808. Astley performed stunts in a 42 ft diameter ring; the standard size used by <a href="/wiki/Circus" title="Circus">circuses</a> ever since.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Circus" title="Circus">circus</a> is a traditional form of entertainment in the UK. <a href="/wiki/Chipperfield%27s_Circus" title="Chipperfield&#39;s Circus">Chipperfield\'s Circus</a> dates back more than 300 years in Britain, making it one of the oldest family circus dynasties.<sup id="cite_ref-CHP_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHP-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philip_Astley" title="Philip Astley">Philip Astley</a> is regarded as the father of the <a href="/wiki/Circus#Development" title="Circus">modern circus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Following his invention of the circus ring in 1768, <a href="/wiki/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre" title="Astley&#39;s Amphitheatre">Astley\'s Amphitheatre</a> opened in London in 1773.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> As an equestrian master Astley had a skill for trick horse-riding, and when he added tumblers, <a href="/wiki/Tightrope" class="mw-redirect" title="Tightrope">tightrope</a>-walkers, <a href="/wiki/Jugglers" class="mw-redirect" title="Jugglers">jugglers</a>, performing dogs, and a <a href="/wiki/Clown" title="Clown">clown</a> to fill time between his own demonstrations \xe2\x80\x93 the modern circus was born.<sup id="cite_ref-BGH_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGH-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The Hughes Royal Circus was popular in London in the 1780s. <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Fanque" title="Pablo Fanque">Pablo Fanque</a>\'s Circus Royal, among the most popular circuses of Victorian England, showcased <a href="/wiki/William_Kite" title="William Kite">William Kite</a>, which inspired <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a> to write "<a href="/wiki/Being_for_the_Benefit_of_Mr._Kite!" title="Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite!">Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite!</a>" on The Beatles\' album <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band" title="Sgt. Pepper&#39;s Lonely Hearts Club Band">Sgt. Pepper\'s Lonely Hearts Club Band</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Grimaldi" title="Joseph Grimaldi">Joseph Grimaldi</a>, the most celebrated of <a href="/wiki/Clown" title="Clown">clowns</a> from England is considered the father of modern clowning.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WarnePantomine1890.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WarnePantomine1890.jpg/220px-WarnePantomine1890.jpg" width="220" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WarnePantomine1890.jpg/330px-WarnePantomine1890.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/WarnePantomine1890.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="570" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WarnePantomine1890.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Christmas Pantomime</i> 1890, UK. <a href="/wiki/Pantomime" title="Pantomime">Pantomime</a> plays a prominent role in British culture during the Christmas and New Year season.<sup id="cite_ref-Panto_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panto-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pantomime" title="Pantomime">Pantomime</a> (often referred to as "panto") is a British <a href="/wiki/Musical_theatre" title="Musical theatre">musical comedy</a> stage production, designed for family entertainment. It is performed in theatres throughout the UK during the Christmas and New Year season. The art originated in the 18th century with <a href="/wiki/John_Weaver_(dancer)" title="John Weaver (dancer)">John Weaver</a>, a dance master and choreographer at the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Royal,_Drury_Lane" title="Theatre Royal, Drury Lane">Theatre Royal, Drury Lane</a> in London.<sup id="cite_ref-Panto_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panto-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In 19th century England it acquired its present form, which includes songs, slapstick comedy and dancing, employing gender-crossing actors, combining topical humour with a story loosely based on a well-known fairy tale.<sup id="cite_ref-Panto_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panto-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> It is a participatory form of theatre, in which the audience sing along with parts of the music and shout out phrases to the performers, such as "It\'s behind you".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">Music hall</a> is a type of British theatrical entertainment popular from the early Victorian era to the mid 20th century. The precursor to variety shows of today, music hall involved a mixture of popular songs, comedy, speciality acts and variety entertainment. British performers who honed their skills at pantomime and music hall sketches include <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stan_Laurel" title="Stan Laurel">Stan Laurel</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Formby" title="George Formby">George Formby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gracie_Fields" title="Gracie Fields">Gracie Fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dan_Leno" title="Dan Leno">Dan Leno</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_Lawrence" title="Gertrude Lawrence">Gertrude Lawrence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Champion" title="Harry Champion">Harry Champion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> British music hall comedian and theatre impresario <a href="/wiki/Fred_Karno" title="Fred Karno">Fred Karno</a> developed a form of sketch comedy without dialogue in the 1890s, and Chaplin and Laurel were among the music hall comedians who worked for him.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> A leading film producer stated; "Fred Karno is not only a genius, he is the man who originated <a href="/wiki/Slapstick" title="Slapstick">slapstick</a> comedy. We in Hollywood owe much to him."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:English_philosophers" title="Category:English philosophers">Category:English philosophers</a></div>\n<p>English philosophers include <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_More" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Thomas More">Sir Thomas More</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_England" title="Religion in England">Religion in England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/200px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" width="200" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/300px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/400px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3113" data-file-height="2395" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a> is the seat of the Primate of All England, the Archbishop of Canterbury, the most senior bishop of the  <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> (it was the property of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> before the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> became the dominant religion in England in the 7th century. <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">Polytheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Indo-European</a> religions, often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">paganism</a>, were practised before Christianity took hold. The most notable of these religions were <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" title="Celtic polytheism">Celtic polytheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_polytheism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman polytheism">Roman polytheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_paganism" title="Anglo-Saxon paganism">Anglo-Saxon paganism</a>, which was the religion of the early English people, or <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, and which was in many ways very similar to the closely related <a href="/wiki/Norse_paganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse paganism">Norse paganism</a> practised by the Scandinavian peoples and that would later be introduced to England by the <a href="/wiki/Danes" title="Danes">Danes</a>.\n</p><p>Christianity was first established in Britain by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. According to legend, Christianity was introduced to Britain by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_of_Arimathea" title="Joseph of Arimathea">Joseph of Arimathea</a>, who came to <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury" title="Glastonbury">Glastonbury</a>. There is also a tradition ascribing this accomplishment to <a href="/wiki/Lucius_of_Britain" title="Lucius of Britain">Lucius of Britain</a>. Archaeological evidence for Christian communities begins to appear in the 3rd and 4th centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Romano-British_culture" title="Romano-British culture">Romano-British</a> population after the withdrawal of the Roman legions remained mostly Christian. The Anglo-Saxon invaders and settlers who replaced them, founding the English nation, represented a stark return to pre-Christian religion for Britain. From the arrival of the Anglo-Saxons beginning in the 4th century until the arrival of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Augustinian_Mission&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Augustinian Mission (page does not exist)">Augustinian Mission</a> in 597 AD, England was entirely pagan, and the pre-Christian Germanic religion was practised openly in pockets throughout the country for many decades after this.\n</p><p>Christianity was reintroduced into England by missionaries from Scotland and from Continental Europe: the era of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Archbishops_of_Canterbury" title="List of Archbishops of Canterbury"> Archbishop of Canterbury</a>,  and the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christian</a> missionaries in the north (notably <a href="/wiki/Aidan_of_Lindisfarne" title="Aidan of Lindisfarne">Aidan of Lindisfarne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">Cuthbert</a>  who came from Scotland) began in 597 AD. The <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Whitby" title="Synod of Whitby">Synod of Whitby</a> in 664 ultimately led to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> being fully part of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>. Early English Christian documents from this time include the 7th-century illuminated <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a> and the historical accounts written by <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Durham_Gospels" title="Durham Gospels">Durham Gospels</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">Gospel</a> book produced at <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a>.\n</p><p>In 1536, the Church of England split from Rome over the issue of the divorce (technically, the marriage annulment) of King <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> from <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>. The split led to the emergence of a separate ecclesiastical authority. Later the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a> resulted in the Church of England adopting its distinctive reformed Catholic position known as <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> which maintains episcopacy while adopting a <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> theology. For more detail of this period see the following article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_English_Reformation" title="Timeline of the English Reformation">Timeline of the English Reformation</a>.\n</p><p>Today, the Church of England is the established church in England. It regards itself as in continuity with the pre-Reformation state Catholic church (something the Roman Catholic Church does not accept) and has been a distinct Anglican church since the settlement under <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I of England</a> (with some disruption during the 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> period). The British Monarch is formally <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor of the Church of England</a>. Its spiritual leader is the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, who is regarded by convention as the head of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>. In practice the Church of England is governed by the <a href="/wiki/General_Synod_of_the_Church_of_England" title="General Synod of the Church of England">General Synod of the Church of England</a>, under the authority of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a>. The Church of England\'s mission to spread the Gospel has seen the establishment of many churches in the Anglican Communion throughout the world particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>.\n</p><p>A strong tradition of <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a> developed from the 18th century onward. The Methodist revival was started in England by a group of men including <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a> and his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wesley" title="Charles Wesley">Charles Wesley</a> as a movement within the Church of England; it developed as a separate denomination after John Wesley\'s death. Other non-conformist Protestant traditions were also established in England.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Saint_George" title="Saint George">Saint George</a> is recognised as the <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of England. Before <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_the_Martyr" title="Edmund the Martyr">Edmund the Martyr</a> was recognised as England\'s patron saint, and the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_England" title="Flag of England">flag of England</a> consists of the <a href="/wiki/Saint_George%27s_Cross" title="Saint George&#39;s Cross">Saint George\'s Cross</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Alban" title="Saint Alban">Saint Alban</a> is venerated by some as England\'s first Christian martyr.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Change_ringing" title="Change ringing">Change ringing</a> is the traditional method of bell ringing in English churches, co-ordinated by the <a href="/wiki/Central_Council_of_Church_Bell_Ringers" title="Central Council of Church Bell Ringers">Central Council of Church Bell Ringers</a><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> and promoted by societies such as the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Society_of_College_Youths" title="Ancient Society of College Youths">Ancient Society of College Youths</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Royal_Cumberland_Youths" title="Society of Royal Cumberland Youths">Society of Royal Cumberland Youths</a>.  Change ringing is central to <a href="/wiki/The_Nine_Tailors" title="The Nine Tailors">The Nine Tailors</a> by <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_L._Sayers" title="Dorothy L. Sayers">Dorothy L. Sayers</a>, voted the best crime novel of the 1930s by the British Crime Writers\' Association.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebration_of_Christmas">Celebration of Christmas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Celebration of Christmas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FatherChristmastrial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/FatherChristmastrial.jpg/180px-FatherChristmastrial.jpg" width="180" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/FatherChristmastrial.jpg/270px-FatherChristmastrial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/FatherChristmastrial.jpg/360px-FatherChristmastrial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="846" data-file-height="978" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FatherChristmastrial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Examination and Trial of <a href="/wiki/Father_Christmas" title="Father Christmas">Father Christmas</a></i> (1686), published after Christmas was reinstated as a holy day in England</div></div></div>\n<p>In 17th-century England, the <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a> condemned the celebration of <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Durston_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durston-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>  In contrast, the Anglican Church "pressed for a more elaborate observance of feasts, penitential seasons, and saints\' days. The calendar reform became a major point of tension between the Anglicans and Puritans."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> also responded, promoting the festival in a more religiously oriented form. King <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I of England</a> directed his noblemen and gentry to return to their landed estates in midwinter to keep up their old-style Christmas generosity. Following the <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarian</a> victory over Charles I during the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, Puritan rulers banned Christmas in 1647.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Protests followed as pro-Christmas rioting broke out in several cities and for weeks <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a> was controlled by the rioters, who decorated doorways with <a href="/wiki/Holly" title="Holly">holly</a> and shouted <a href="/wiki/Royalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Royalism">royalist</a> slogans.<sup id="cite_ref-Durston_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durston-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The book, <i>The Vindication of Christmas</i> (London, 1652), argued against the Puritans, and makes note of Old English Christmas traditions, dinner, roast apples on the fire, card playing, dances with "plow-boys" and "maidservants", old Father Christmas and carol singing.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">Restoration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King Charles II</a> in 1660 ended the ban. Following the Restoration, <i>Poor Robin\'s Almanack</i> contained the lines: "Now thanks to God for Charles return, / Whose absence made old Christmas mourn. / For then we scarcely did it know, / Whether it Christmas were or no."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Charles_Dickens_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/180px-Charles_Dickens_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" width="180" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Charles_Dickens_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/270px-Charles_Dickens_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/22/Charles_Dickens_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg 2x" data-file-width="278" data-file-height="299" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Dickens_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>\' 1843 novella <i><a href="/wiki/A_Christmas_Carol" title="A Christmas Carol">A Christmas Carol</a></i> played a major role in reviving the "spirit" of Christmas and seasonal goodwill</div></div></div>\n<p>In the early 19th century, writers imagined <a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">Tudor</a> Christmas as a time of heartfelt celebration. In 1843, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> wrote the novel <i><a href="/wiki/A_Christmas_Carol" title="A Christmas Carol">A Christmas Carol</a></i> that helped revive the "spirit" of Christmas and seasonal merriment.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Dickens sought to construct Christmas as a family-centred festival of generosity, in contrast to the community-based and church-centred observations, the observance of which had dwindled during the late 18th century and early 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Dickens influenced many aspects of Christmas that are celebrated today in Western culture, such as family gatherings, <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_food" title="Seasonal food">seasonal food</a> and drink, dancing, games, and a festive generosity of spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> A prominent phrase from the tale, <a href="/wiki/Christmas_and_holiday_season#History_of_the_phrase" title="Christmas and holiday season">"Merry Christmas"</a>, was popularized following the appearance of the story.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The term <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Scrooge" title="Ebenezer Scrooge">Scrooge</a> became a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Miser" title="Miser">miser</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Humbug" title="Humbug">"Bah! Humbug!"</a> dismissive of the festive spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The revival of the <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Carol" class="mw-redirect" title="Christmas Carol">Christmas Carol</a> began with <a href="/wiki/William_Sandys_(antiquarian)" title="William Sandys (antiquarian)">William Sandys</a>\'s <i>Christmas Carols Ancient and Modern</i> (1833), with the first appearance in print of "<a href="/wiki/The_First_Noel" title="The First Noel">The First Noel</a>", "<a href="/wiki/I_Saw_Three_Ships" title="I Saw Three Ships">I Saw Three Ships</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Hark_the_Herald_Angels_Sing" class="mw-redirect" title="Hark the Herald Angels Sing">Hark the Herald Angels Sing</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/God_Rest_Ye_Merry,_Gentlemen" class="mw-redirect" title="God Rest Ye Merry, Gentlemen">God Rest Ye Merry, Gentlemen</a>". In 1843 the first commercial <a href="/wiki/Christmas_card#History" title="Christmas card">Christmas card</a> was produced by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cole" title="Henry Cole">Henry Cole</a> leading to the exchange of festive greeting cards among the public.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_markers:_blue_plaques">Historical markers: blue plaques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Historical markers: blue plaques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_(4313226125).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_%284313226125%29.jpg/210px-Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_%284313226125%29.jpg" width="210" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_%284313226125%29.jpg/315px-Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_%284313226125%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_%284313226125%29.jpg/420px-Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_%284313226125%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Alfred_Hitchcock_(4313226125).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>English Heritage <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> commemorating Sir <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a> at 153 Cromwell Road, London</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a>, the oldest <a href="/wiki/Historical_marker" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical marker">historical marker</a> scheme in the world, is a permanent sign installed in a public place in the UK to commemorate a link between that location and a famous person or event.<sup id="cite_ref-Blue_plaque_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_plaque-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The scheme was the brainchild of politician <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_(British_politician)" title="William Ewart (British politician)">William Ewart</a> in 1863 and was initiated in 1866.<sup id="cite_ref-Blue_plaque_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_plaque-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> It was formally established by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Society of Arts">Society of Arts</a> in 1867, and since 1986 has been run by <a href="/wiki/English_Heritage" title="English Heritage">English Heritage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Blue_plaque_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_plaque-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first plaque was unveiled in 1867 to commemorate <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a> at his birthplace, 24 Holles Street, <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Square" title="Cavendish Square">Cavendish Square</a>, London. Examples that commemorate events include <a href="/wiki/John_Logie_Baird" title="John Logie Baird">John Logie Baird</a>\'s first demonstration of the television at 22 <a href="/wiki/Frith_Street" title="Frith Street">Frith Street</a>, Westminster, W1, London, and the first <a href="/wiki/Four-minute_mile" title="Four-minute mile">sub 4-minute mile</a> run by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bannister" title="Roger Bannister">Roger Bannister</a> on 6 May 1954 at Oxford University\'s <a href="/wiki/Iffley_Road_Track" class="mw-redirect" title="Iffley Road Track">Iffley Road Track</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg/220px-Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg/330px-Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg/440px-Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yard" title="Yard">Yard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">foot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inch" title="Inch">inch</a> measurements erected at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich" title="Royal Observatory, Greenwich">Royal Observatory</a>, London in the 19th century. Today the English public commonly measure distance in <a href="/wiki/Miles" class="mw-redirect" title="Miles">miles</a> and yards, height in feet and inches, weight in <a href="/wiki/Stone_(unit)" title="Stone (unit)">stone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pounds_(mass)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pounds (mass)">pounds</a>, speed in <a href="/wiki/Miles_per_hour" title="Miles per hour">miles per hour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>England was a leading centre of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a> since the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The English have played a significant role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>. Prominent individuals have included <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isambard_Kingdom_Brunel" title="Isambard Kingdom Brunel">Isambard Kingdom Brunel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Babbage" title="Charles Babbage">Charles Babbage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_I" title="Abraham Darby I">Abraham Darby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">Joseph Swan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnes_Wallis" title="Barnes Wallis">Barnes Wallis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Whittle" title="Frank Whittle">Frank Whittle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee" title="Tim Berners-Lee">Tim Berners-Lee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>. It is home to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Observatory" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenwich Observatory">Greenwich Observatory</a> and its associated <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">meridian</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_Revolution">Industrial Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Industrial Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> began in England due to the social, economic and political changes implemented in the previous centuries. Whereas <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> stayed the normal form of power execution through most parts of Europe, institutions ensured property rights and political safety to British people after the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688. Aided by these legal and cultural foundations, an <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurial" class="mw-redirect" title="Entrepreneurial">entrepreneurial</a> spirit and consumer revolution drove industrialisation in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>  Geographical and natural resource advantages of Great Britain also contributed, with the country\'s extensive coast lines and many navigable rivers in an age where water was the easiest means of transportation. Britain also had high quality coal. According to British historian <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Black_(historian)" title="Jeremy Black (historian)">Jeremy Black</a>, "an unprecedented explosion of new ideas, and new technological inventions, transformed our use of energy, creating an increasingly industrial and urbanised country. Roads, railways and canals were built. Great cities appeared. Scores of factories and mills sprang up. Our landscape would never be the same again. It was a revolution that transformed not only the country, but the world itself."<sup id="cite_ref-Black_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg/220px-Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg" width="220" height="323" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg/330px-Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg/440px-Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1088" data-file-height="1598" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Josiah_Wedgwood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood" title="Josiah Wedgwood">Josiah Wedgwood</a> was a leading entrepreneur in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The 18th century entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood" title="Josiah Wedgwood">Josiah Wedgwood</a> is credited with the industrialisation of the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>. Meeting the demands of the consumer revolution and growth in wealth of the middle classes in Britain, Wedgwood created goods such as <a href="/wiki/Tableware" title="Tableware">tableware</a>, which was starting to become a common feature on dining tables.<sup id="cite_ref-Black_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Credited as the inventor of modern <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a>, Wedgwood pioneered <a href="/wiki/Direct_marketing" title="Direct marketing">direct mail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Money_back_guarantee" title="Money back guarantee">money back guarantees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Door-to-door" title="Door-to-door">travelling salesmen</a>, carrying pattern boxes for display, <a href="/wiki/Self-service" title="Self-service">self-service</a>, free delivery, <a href="/wiki/Buy_one,_get_one_free" title="Buy one, get one free">buy one get one free</a>, and illustrated catalogues.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Described as "natural capitalists" by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>, dynasties of <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a> were successful in business matters which contributed the Industrial Revolution. This included ironmaking by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_I" title="Abraham Darby I">Abraham Darby I</a> and his family; banking, including <a href="/wiki/Lloyds_Banking_Group" title="Lloyds Banking Group">Lloyds Banking Group</a> (founded by <a href="/wiki/Sampson_Lloyd" title="Sampson Lloyd">Sampson Lloyd</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Quaker_Enterprise_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quaker_Enterprise-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barclays_PLC" class="mw-redirect" title="Barclays PLC">Barclays PLC</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Quaker_Enterprise_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quaker_Enterprise-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Backhouse%27s_Bank" title="Backhouse&#39;s Bank">Backhouse\'s Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gurney%27s_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurney&#39;s Bank">Gurney\'s Bank</a>; life assurance (<a href="/wiki/Friends_Provident" title="Friends Provident">Friends Provident</a>); pharmaceuticals (<a href="/wiki/Allen_%26_Hanburys" title="Allen &amp; Hanburys">Allen &amp; Hanburys</a><sup id="cite_ref-Quaker_Enterprise_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quaker_Enterprise-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>); the big three British chocolate companies <a href="/wiki/Cadbury" title="Cadbury">Cadbury</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Quaker_Enterprise_133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quaker_Enterprise-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._S._Fry_%26_Sons" title="J. S. Fry &amp; Sons">Fry\'s</a><sup id="cite_ref-Quaker_Enterprise_133-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quaker_Enterprise-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rowntree%27s" title="Rowntree&#39;s">Rowntree</a><sup id="cite_ref-Quaker_Enterprise_133-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quaker_Enterprise-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>); biscuit manufacturing (<a href="/wiki/Huntley_%26_Palmers" title="Huntley &amp; Palmers">Huntley &amp; Palmers</a><sup id="cite_ref-Quaker_Enterprise_133-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quaker_Enterprise-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>); match manufacture (<a href="/wiki/Bryant_and_May" title="Bryant and May">Bryant and May</a>) and shoe manufacturing (<a href="/wiki/C._%26_J._Clark" title="C. &amp; J. Clark">Clarks</a>). With his role in the marketing and manufacturing of <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>\'s steam engine, and invention of modern <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Boulton" title="Matthew Boulton">Matthew Boulton</a> is regarded as one of the most influential entrepreneurs in history.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Other important English engineers and inventors in the Industrial Revolution include; <a href="/wiki/George_Stephenson" title="George Stephenson">George Stephenson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Arkwright" title="Richard Arkwright">Richard Arkwright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Maudslay" title="Henry Maudslay">Henry Maudslay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isambard_Kingdom_Brunel" title="Isambard Kingdom Brunel">Isambard Kingdom Brunel</a>. England has the oldest <a href="/wiki/Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Railway">railway</a> networks in the world, with the <a href="/wiki/Stockton_and_Darlington_Railway" title="Stockton and Darlington Railway">Stockton and Darlington Railway</a>, opened in 1825, the first public railway to use steam locomotives.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Opened in 1863, <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a> is the world\'s first underground railway.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Known as the "Father of Railways", Stephenson\'s <a href="/wiki/Track_gauge#The_Stockton_and_Darlington_Railway" title="Track gauge">rail gauge</a> of 4&#160;feet <span class="frac nowrap">8<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>&#160;inches (1,435&#160;mm) is the <a href="/wiki/Standard_gauge" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard gauge">standard gauge</a> for most of the world\'s <a href="/wiki/Railways" class="mw-redirect" title="Railways">railways</a>. Henry Maudslay\'s most influential invention was the <a href="/wiki/Screw-cutting_lathe" title="Screw-cutting lathe">screw-cutting lathe</a>, a machine which created uniformity in <a href="/wiki/Screw" title="Screw">screws</a> and allowed for the application of <a href="/wiki/Interchangeable_parts" title="Interchangeable parts">interchangeable parts</a> (a prerequisite for <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a>): it was a revolutionary development necessary for the Industrial Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Quentin_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quentin-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Brunel created the <a href="/wiki/Great_Western_Railway" title="Great Western Railway">Great Western Railway</a>, as well as famous steamships including the <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Great_Britain" title="SS Great Britain">SS Great Britain</a>,</i> the first propeller-driven ocean-going iron ship, and <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Great_Eastern" title="SS Great Eastern">SS Great Eastern</a></i> which laid the first lasting <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_telegraph_cable" title="Transatlantic telegraph cable">transatlantic telegraph cable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport_and_leisure">Sport and leisure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sport and leisure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_England" title="Sport in England">Sport in England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Champions_statue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Champions_statue.jpg/220px-Champions_statue.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Champions_statue.jpg/330px-Champions_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Champions_statue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Champions_statue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/World_Cup_Sculpture" title="World Cup Sculpture">World Cup Sculpture</a></i> featuring captain <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Moore" title="Bobby Moore">Bobby Moore</a> with the 1966 World Cup trophy, on the shoulders of <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Hurst" title="Geoff Hurst">Geoff Hurst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ray_Wilson_(English_footballer)" title="Ray Wilson (English footballer)">Ray Wilson</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Martin_Peters" title="Martin Peters">Martin Peters</a></div></div></div>\n<p>There are many sports which have been codified by the English, and then spread worldwide, including <a href="/wiki/Badminton" title="Badminton">badminton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croquet" title="Croquet">croquet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Football_(soccer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Football (soccer)">football</a>, <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">field hockey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lawn_tennis" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawn tennis">lawn tennis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby league</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Table_tennis" title="Table tennis">table tennis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thoroughbred_horse_race" class="mw-redirect" title="Thoroughbred horse race">thoroughbred horse racing</a>. In the late 18th century, the English game of <a href="/wiki/Rounders" title="Rounders">rounders</a> was transported to the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonies" class="mw-redirect" title="American Colonies">American Colonies</a>, where it evolved into <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a>. <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Association football</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby league</a> are considered to be the national sports of England.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_the_Game_(association_football)" title="Laws of the Game (association football)">rules</a> of football were first drafted in 1863 by <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Cobb_Morley" title="Ebenezer Cobb Morley">Ebenezer Cobb Morley</a>, and England has the oldest football clubs in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Recognised by <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a> as the birthplace of club football, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_F.C." title="Sheffield F.C.">Sheffield F.C.</a>, founded in 1857, is the world\'s oldest football club.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The first ever international football match was between <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland_national_football_team" title="Scotland national football team">Scotland</a> in 1872.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Referred to by the sport\'s governing body FIFA as the "home of football", England hosted the <a href="/wiki/1966_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1966 FIFA World Cup">1966 FIFA World Cup</a>, and won the tournament.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> With a 32.3 million viewing audience, <a href="/wiki/1966_FIFA_World_Cup_Final" title="1966 FIFA World Cup Final">the 1966 final</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_most-watched_television_broadcasts#Most_watched_special_events" title="List of most-watched television broadcasts">most watched television event ever</a> in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The English <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Barclays Premier League</a> is the most-watched football league in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Its biggest clubs include <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Hotspur" class="mw-redirect" title="Tottenham Hotspur">Tottenham Hotspur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_F.C." title="Manchester City F.C.">Manchester City</a>. In 2016, <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_F.C." title="Leicester City F.C.">Leicester City</a>, who were 5,000-1 outsiders at the start of the season, became champions.<sup id="cite_ref-outsiders_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-outsiders-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> By some measures it was the greatest sporting upset ever: multiple bookmakers had never paid out at such long odds for any sport.<sup id="cite_ref-outsiders_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-outsiders-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>  As is the case throughout the UK, football in England is renowned for the intense rivalries between clubs and the passion of the supporters, which includes a tradition of football chants, such as, "<a href="/wiki/You%27re_Not_Singing_Any_More" title="You&#39;re Not Singing Any More">You\'re Not Singing Any More</a>" (or its variant "We Can See You Sneaking Out!"), sung by jubilant fans towards the opposition fans who have gone silent (or left early).<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Court_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Court_1.jpg/220px-Court_1.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Court_1.jpg/330px-Court_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Court_1.jpg/440px-Court_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Court_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First played in 1877, the <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon</a> Championships is the oldest tennis tournament in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNTennis_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNTennis-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The modern game of <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a> originated in Birmingham, England, in the 1860s,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> and after its creation, tennis spread throughout the upper-class English-speaking population, before spreading around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Major <a href="/wiki/Walter_Clopton_Wingfield" title="Walter Clopton Wingfield">Walter Clopton Wingfield</a> is credited as being a pioneer of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The world\'s oldest tennis tournament, the <a href="/wiki/Wimbledon_championships" class="mw-redirect" title="Wimbledon championships">Wimbledon championships</a>, first occurred in 1877, and today the event takes place over two weeks in late June and early July.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNTennis_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNTennis-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Created in the Tudor period in the court of Henry VIII, the English dessert strawberries and cream is synonymous with the British summer, and is famously consumed at Wimbledon.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:England_world_cup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/England_world_cup.jpg/220px-England_world_cup.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/England_world_cup.jpg/330px-England_world_cup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/England_world_cup.jpg/440px-England_world_cup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1519" data-file-height="1144" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:England_world_cup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Celebrations at <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> after England\'s <a href="/wiki/2003_Rugby_World_Cup" title="2003 Rugby World Cup">2003 Rugby World Cup</a> victory.</div></div></div>\n<p>England, and other countries in the United Kingdom, compete as a separate nations in some international sporting events, especially in football, cricket, rugby league and rugby union. The <a href="/wiki/England_cricket_team" title="England cricket team">England cricket team</a> actually represents England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-Cricket_World_Cup_2007_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cricket_World_Cup_2007-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> However, in the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>, England competes as part of the Great Britain team. English supporters are now more likely to carry the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_England" title="Flag of England">Cross of Saint George flag</a> than the British <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Daily_Mirror17.09.05_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Mirror17.09.05-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Football_in_England" title="Football in England">Football</a> maintains a consistent popularity across the country and is often indicative of trends across wider culture in England, such as in clothing and music. Different sports directly represent the different social classes within England. Rugby league, for instance, was traditionally associated with the old <a href="/wiki/Mill_towns" class="mw-redirect" title="Mill towns">mill towns</a> of north-west England, whereas cricket and rugby union have their origins in the private schools of the 18th and 19th centuries respectively.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_England.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/150px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" width="150" height="90" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/225px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/300px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_England.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The English flag.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Arms_of_England.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Royal_Arms_of_England.svg/170px-Royal_Arms_of_England.svg.png" width="170" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Royal_Arms_of_England.svg/255px-Royal_Arms_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Royal_Arms_of_England.svg/340px-Royal_Arms_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="478" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Arms_of_England.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "three lions", the second Great Seal of  <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">King Richard the Lionheart</a> (1198)</div></div></div>\n<p>The English use as their national flag the red cross of <a href="/wiki/St_George" class="mw-redirect" title="St George">St George</a>. <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Day_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="St George&#39;s Day in England">St George\'s Day in England</a> is marked as the day of the patron saint, and is also celebrated as the day of birth and death of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>.\n</p><p>In 1198, <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">King Richard the Lionheart</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Arms_of_England" title="Royal Arms of England">coat of arms of England</a>, depicting "three lions".<sup id="cite_ref-ogh_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ogh-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The three lions form the basis of several emblems of English national sports teams, such as the <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England national football team</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/England_cricket_team" title="England cricket team">English national cricket team</a> (though in blue rather than gold). The English <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor rose</a> are also English symbols, the latter of which is (although more modernised) used by the <a href="/wiki/England_national_rugby_union_team" title="England national rugby union team">England national rugby union team</a>.\n</p><p>England has no official <a href="/wiki/Anthem" title="Anthem">anthem</a>; however, the United Kingdom\'s "<a href="/wiki/God_Save_the_Queen" title="God Save the Queen">God Save the Queen</a>" is commonly used. Other songs are sometimes used, including "<a href="/wiki/Land_of_Hope_and_Glory" title="Land of Hope and Glory">Land of Hope and Glory</a>" (used as England\'s anthem in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>), "<a href="/wiki/And_did_those_feet_in_ancient_time" title="And did those feet in ancient time">Jerusalem</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Rule,_Britannia!" title="Rule, Britannia!">Rule Britannia</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/I_Vow_to_Thee,_My_Country" title="I Vow to Thee, My Country">I Vow to Thee, My Country</a>". Moves by certain groups are encouraging adoption of an official English anthem following similar occurrences in Scotland and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See
